# Original Post
**URL:** https://www.askamanager.org/2025/07/my-boss-said-im-threatened-by-his-masculine-energy.html
**Page Title:** my boss said I'm threatened by his "masculine energy" - Ask a Manager
--------------------

A reader writes:
I am a Millennial woman and my new boss is a Gen X man. We have been butting heads a lot, mostly because I think he lacks the basic skills and competencies to do his job. My frustration has gotten to the point where I feel like screaming most days.
This past week I had to send him yet another email where I tried to politely and professionally explain that he was yet again doing something wrong. I had two people read it for tone before I sent it. This is the opening paragraph to the 10 paragraphs he sent in response:
“I think [Name] that you would benefit from learning about the unconscious and the psychological defense of projections and transferences that emanate from the unconscious of a person, especially one with a highly dysregulated nervous system. I am a human being too — I have done it and can do it (still do it at times) and that’s why I know about it experientially. It’s also why I speak to the need for grace often (as well as accountability). Believe it or not (and that is a literal statement because I really don’t think you can believe it at this point in your life), I extend a great deal of grace to you. But that does not mean I am going to take on crap that you are trying to offload on to me. Nor am I going to just be a wallflower as a director of an organization that needs to address its challenges. Because you have been working in an all-female environment for so long, it’s quite possible that you (and others) take the masculine energy that I at times emanate as a threat, when there is no threat. But you perceive it as so. I’m sorry about that and I can be mindful of behaviors but I am not going to sit in analysis paralysis while we try to adjust to the chaos left behind in the emotional wake of the Trump Train.”
The best part about this email is that he voluntarily cc’d the board chair on it. He tries to paint me as a hysterical, flakey, incompetent woman, which fell flat because I’ve worked with our chair, a man, for the last 10+ years.
A few weeks prior to this email, I had asked an external project partner if I could use him as a professional reference as he has had nothing but very nice things to say about the work I’ve done with him for the last 3+ years. The day after I received this unhinged email from my boss, that project partner called me and asked how my job search was going. I said “not great,” and he asked if I wanted to come work with him. We later had a two-hour long conversation, and I’m being offered a pay bump and an opportunity to oversee a really awesome project.
So, now I need to write my resignation letter to my boss. Due to our summer PTO schedules, I won’t actually see my boss for another 2.5 weeks, and I won’t be starting my new job until mid-August. When he gets back to the office, I would love to have a polite and professional response composed that burns this man and his “masculine emanations” to the ground. Can you offer me any advice on what to say?
P.S. I spoke to an employment attorney and because our organization has fewer than 15 employees, it’s not required to comply with federal anti-discrimination laws. I apparently don’t have a lot of legal rights in this instance. While this is bonkers, I am working to put together additional documentation for the board that will hopefully inspire them to fire him.
Eww, my skin crawls every time I try to read the paragraph he wrote to you. From the bottom of my heart, thank you for not sending the other nine paragraphs.
I don’t think the issue is this guy’s “masculine energy.” It’s that he seems incapable of engaging on actual work issues and instead wants to psychoanalyze you and conclude that you just don’t know how to work around masculine men because of all that estrogen you’ve been steeping in.
I don’t have any real context for what’s been going on in your office or what he refers to as “crap that you are trying to offload on (him),” and an effective response would probably require knowing some of that.
But in this case, you really don’t need to respond at all! You’re leaving. This isn’t someone who’s engaging in good faith or in a productive way, and you’re on your way out. There’s no reason you need to work toward a greater understanding with him so you can both move forward, and there’s little that indicates that would succeed even if you wanted to try. So why bother?
Your resignation letter itself should be bland and dry, as should all resignation letters . They’re not meant to have any meaningful content at all beyond, “I am resigning and my last day will be X.”
As for responding to his ludicrous email, if you respond at all, at most you should say, “This email is inappropriate on multiple levels, and you should not be applying this sort of explicitly gendered lens to work interactions. I do not think it will be productive to discuss this further, so I will leave this with BoardChair to handle from here.”
Read an update to this letter .
- my ex-boss threatened to contact my husband, his coworkers, and my father-in-law if I don't return $48 of office supplies
- my boss asked if I felt "threatened" by a new hire when I complained about her
- is it fair to give no notice when your boss has threatened to push resigning employees out early?

About 15 years ago I was sitting in on a portion of an interview where the interviewee never broke eye contact with me. There were probably three or four other in that conference room, all asking questions, and this was a “get to know your colleagues in separate-but-collaborative departments” meeting, and the dude never looked away when others asked questions/addressed all his answers to me. My colleagues clearly saw what he was doing and were more weirded out than I was: after a few minutes I knew there was no way this guy would get hired and the absurdity seemed bespoke for my sense of humor. I stared right back to see how long it would go. I have NO IDEA what this guy thought he was gaining by attempting to stare me down (and no, he didn’t do this to anyone else in any other portion of the interview). I wish I remembered the gender breakdown, but it was during the portion of my career when I was always the youngest-looking woman in the room (sigh, I do not miss that!). ▼ Collapse 4 replies Yeah, and even moreso – I want to be part of a mixed group of interviewers. I wish I remembered the gender breakdown, but it was during the portion of my career when I was always the youngest-looking woman in the room (sigh, I do not miss that!). ▼ Collapse 4 replies Yeah, and even moreso – I want to be part of a mixed group of interviewers. Some people might have a slightly odd vibe when interviewing with just women, but when you get a mix of appearances/presentations in the same room, the sexism is so much more likely to come out one way or another. ▼ Collapse 2 replies We had a candidate who was fine with a group that was all women and a mixed panel, and then aggressively bro-ed out when 1:1 with a guy. From then on we made sure to have 3 very specifically curated panels. ▼ Collapse 1 reply Not long after I started at ToxicExjob (before it was super toxic), my original boss had a male candidate come in for a sales job, or something, I forget. The interview didn’t last very long — after he left, she came out and said he rolled his eyes when he saw her. She and I and her assistant all agreed he was a Level 1 Nope. I had a session with a male interviewee talking to myself (female) and one other engineer (male). At the beginning of the interview, we very clearly introduced ourselves — me as Director of Engineering, and my coworker as a software engineer. Every time I asked the candidate a question, he delivered the answer to my male coworker. Then there was the time a female colleague and I ran a session with a male candidate who was, wait for it, at least a decade older than me. He was practically appalled when I actually expected him to outline a little bit of code on the white board. And when he did, and I asked a whole bunch of leading questions pointing to a bug in it, he treated me like I couldn’t possibly understand his brilliant code….until I made him walk through it, line by line, and he encountered the inevitable error. He then paused, erased a bit, fixed it, and then went on like nothing had happened. Spoilers: neither of those candidates were offered a job with us.
YES, re: “some dudes can’t be normal at women”! About 15 years ago I was sitting in on a portion of an interview where the interviewee never broke eye contact with me. There were probably three or four other in that conference room, all asking questions, and this was a “get to know your colleagues in separate-but-collaborative departments” meeting, and the dude never looked away when others asked questions/addressed all his answers to me. My colleagues clearly saw what he was doing and were more weirded out than I was: after a few minutes I knew there was no way this guy would get hired and the absurdity seemed bespoke for my sense of humor. I stared right back to see how long it would go. I have NO IDEA what this guy thought he was gaining by attempting to stare me down (and no, he didn’t do this to anyone else in any other portion of the interview). I wish I remembered the gender breakdown, but it was during the portion of my career when I was always the youngest-looking woman in the room (sigh, I do not miss that!).

I guess the big gap here is what’s been going on at your end. don’t get me wrong – even if you have been 100% incorrect this response is wildly inappropriate. It sounds like concerns you have are well founded. However: “I had to send him yet another email where I tried to politely and professionally explain that he was yet again doing something wrong.” Did ring a few alarm bells for me. If these errors are legal, moral or safety based then of course you must persist. Equally if they are not but are clearly objectively errors you should raise them (politely). However if it’s otherwise a disagreement in approach you sort of have to disagree once and then commit. Scratching the itch of showing up your boss’ limitations by writing repeated complaints (that you get others in your office to read?) is never going to end well. For what it’s worth, I don’t see the letter-writer saying this guy is out of line because he’s Gen X anywhere. I think the generations are in there just as some standard background info. The best revenge is to forget it immediately, and if asked about it, be very shruggy and nonchalant to the point you’re not sure you even read it. OP you don’t need to point out the obvious fool in the room, just focus all your attention on the great new job!

- OP: Gotchas don’t work. Evidence: His 10 paragraph email to you was supposed to be a gotcha. There was a whole glorious scripted response in his head, that you were going to have in response to each brilliantly wrought line. And yet it spectacularly failed. Just write a slim, professional letter of resignation, free of emanations and literallys. Think of it as an escaped llama slammed into your break room and knocked over the garbage and is now rolling around in the effluvia while delivering zingers, and rather than try to get down there in the banana peels with it while delivering your own zingers, you calmly say “Right. I’m out” and leave. ▼ Collapse 11 replies Truly love your llama analogy, which is somehow still leagues more appealing than the actual situation. @LW, in addition to the recommended short resignation letter, why not use the extra fuel to plan something really nice for yourself? Josh is my hero You are totally right, this was supposed to be a gotcha from his end and it did totally fail…I have long since realized that gotcha’s do not work (and if I find myself writing more than 6 sentences of emotionally driven response, I generally try to see how truly necessary/helpful that response is going to be). OP: Gotchas don’t work. Evidence: His 10 paragraph email to you was supposed to be a gotcha. There was a whole glorious scripted response in his head, that you were going to have in response to each brilliantly wrought line. And yet it spectacularly failed.
Just write a slim, professional letter of resignation, free of emanations and literallys. Think of it as an escaped llama slammed into your break room and knocked over the garbage and is now rolling around in the effluvia while delivering zingers, and rather than try to get down there in the banana peels with it while delivering your own zingers, you calmly say “Right. I’m out” and leave.

- In my experience, people who send ten-paragraph emails are very much hoping you will respond with an email of similar length and they want to rope you into a protracted back-and-forth argument. Well, it takes two people to argue. At most I’d respond with: noted. That’s it. All lower case, and no punctuation (you want to demonstrate you are expending as little energy on this person as possible). I’m also a fan of responding to stuff like this with “k” but that implies agreement, so never use that when you aren’t intending to communicate that you agree with what has been said. - Aha! I was once the recipient of a multi-paragraph email from an older man all about how I was “bullying” him because I was “threatened” by him and could not possibly understand everything he had to teach me because he was sooooo much older and experienced, and he copied our supervisor. There were some fun nuances (we were both medical students; I found out a few weeks later that he contacted several of my colleagues to complain about what a “bitch” and “bully” I am; I found a year later that he’d sexually harrassed multiple colleagues of multiple genders and also slept with one of our teachers and also apparently just didn’t show up for an entire rotation and also made sexualized comments about patients.*) I also knew that I would be stuck spending about 18 hours a week with him for another three months, so I couldn’t say everything I wanted to. Later, I was involved in bringing all of the above behaviour to the attention of the administration. I got a lot of, “We take this seriously but we cannot tell you anything about what we’ll do with this information.” Sometimes people like That Guy fail upwards. But in my case, the guy never matched to a residency program and has therefore not been able to work as a physician. It was not within my power to change him, but it was within my power to bring his behaviour to the attention of others. You don’t need to make a fool of this guy; he’s made a fool of himself in front of a board member. Send the suggested reply, a barebones resignation letter, and forward the exchange to the board with a dead-serious, “I wanted to bring to your attention the email that I received from [Name.] Needless to say, this was completely inappropriate and certainly not in keeping wtih the values and professional conduct taht I’ve experienced in my 10 years working at [Company.]” Then, let it go and live your life. ▼ Collapse 2 replies ooof.  there’s not many people I would wish getting kicked out of medical school, but I’m glad at least his behavior + your reporting it eventually stopped him from getting more power/authority over people D: ▼ Collapse 1 reply Thanks, “ooof” is right. My advice to the OP is to bring his actions to the attention of people who can issue consequences, and then try to be at peace with the fact that you did everything you could to stop him from treating others the same way.

- OP, I’m a big fan of sparse resignation letters -and they can be satisfying in their own way! At my last job, which had been a clustermess of bullying and whatnot (the director would literally give me food I was allergic to for my birthday), I turned in a resignation letter that simply said “I am resigning and my last day of work will be X,” and NOTHING more than that. My bosses were in a tizzy for weeks trying to figure out where I was going, talking to all my coworkers to try to find information, and being super nice to me until I left. They even asked me directly why I didn’t say anything else in my letter, and I told them “It has all the information you need.” It was a VERY satisfying and professional revenge. ▼ Collapse 3 replies I love this lol Yup. OP, I’m a big fan of sparse resignation letters -and they can be satisfying in their own way! At my last job, which had been a clustermess of bullying and whatnot (the director would literally give me food I was allergic to for my birthday), I turned in a resignation letter that simply said “I am resigning and my last day of work will be X,” and NOTHING more than that. My bosses were in a tizzy for weeks trying to figure out where I was going, talking to all my coworkers to try to find information, and being super nice to me until I left. They even asked me directly why I didn’t say anything else in my letter, and I told them “It has all the information you need.” It was a VERY satisfying and professional revenge.

- Others have offered excellent advice to not respond, but if you really feel compelled to, I’ll share what I did in a similar situation/ The background is that I had a HUGE conflict with one of my parents about a decade ago over some typical family dynamic stuff. I sent a fairly short, careful email to my parent to try to find some common ground on an argument we’d had. I apologized for my part, asked to discuss the places we disagreed, etc. They responded with a 3-page rant about how ungrateful I am and listing ways in which I had personally offended them in the past two years. About a quarter of the items were true but (in my opinion) blown out of proportion, half were taken wildly out of context, and a quarter never happened at all. I spent an hour or two writing a long and wild response in Microsoft Word just to get the thoughts out of my head…and then I deleted it and didn’t send it. Instead, I responded to that email with something like, “We are clearly on very different pages here, and I don’t think continuing to discuss this right now would be productive. Perhaps it’s best to shelve the issue for now and pick it back up when we’ve both calmed down a bit.” My relationship with my parent didn’t get fixed by that email, they didn’t realize the error in their ways…but I did make clear that I was not going to engage with their bizarre rant, and they didn’t send me another one in response (your mileage may vary).
Others have offered excellent advice to not respond, but if you really feel compelled to, I’ll share what I did in a similar situation/ The background is that I had a HUGE conflict with one of my parents about a decade ago over some typical family dynamic stuff. I sent a fairly short, careful email to my parent to try to find some common ground on an argument we’d had. I apologized for my part, asked to discuss the places we disagreed, etc. They responded with a 3-page rant about how ungrateful I am and listing ways in which I had personally offended them in the past two years. About a quarter of the items were true but (in my opinion) blown out of proportion, half were taken wildly out of context, and a quarter never happened at all. I spent an hour or two writing a long and wild response in Microsoft Word just to get the thoughts out of my head…and then I deleted it and didn’t send it.
Instead, I responded to that email with something like, “We are clearly on very different pages here, and I don’t think continuing to discuss this right now would be productive. Perhaps it’s best to shelve the issue for now and pick it back up when we’ve both calmed down a bit.”
My relationship with my parent didn’t get fixed by that email, they didn’t realize the error in their ways…but I did make clear that I was not going to engage with their bizarre rant, and they didn’t send me another one in response (your mileage may vary).

- OP, I don’t know if you’ll see this because there are already hundreds of comments. But your letter reminded me of patterns I’ve struggled with in the past and I want to see if this resonates: This part of your letter stuck out to me “This past week I had to send him yet another email where I tried to politely and professionally explain that he was yet again doing something wrong.” As Alison said, we don’t have the context to assess this much and I’m going to assume you are correct that the problem was on his end. But why was it on you to inform him of that? Why not *his* supervisor? I ask because you specifically phrased it as “doing something wrong” as opposed to “doing something that inhibited the ability to do my job” or whatever else might be relevant to you. I see a connection between this and your impulse to write a scathing resignation letter that puts him in his place. Both, to me, indicate that you feel responsible in some way for curbing this guy’s bad behavior, and the idea that if you just explain things the right way, it’ll make him stop. I’m here to let you know that not only is it not your job to give him feedback on his performance, you don’t have the ability to change what he does. Only he does. His superiors may have the power to enforce consequences if he doesn’t change but even they can’t control him. You are completely right for wanting out of there and rejecting his nonsense email. But on your way out, I’d do some self reflection on whether the tendency to take responsibility for your manager/supervisor is a norm this particular workplace enforced on you or if it’s a longer standing habit you’ve applied in other parts of life, that may follow you to future work places.
OP, I don’t know if you’ll see this because there are already hundreds of comments. But your letter reminded me of patterns I’ve struggled with in the past and I want to see if this resonates:
This part of your letter stuck out to me “This past week I had to send him yet another email where I tried to politely and professionally explain that he was yet again doing something wrong.” As Alison said, we don’t have the context to assess this much and I’m going to assume you are correct that the problem was on his end. But why was it on you to inform him of that? Why not *his* supervisor? I ask because you specifically phrased it as “doing something wrong” as opposed to “doing something that inhibited the ability to do my job” or whatever else might be relevant to you.
I see a connection between this and your impulse to write a scathing resignation letter that puts him in his place. Both, to me, indicate that you feel responsible in some way for curbing this guy’s bad behavior, and the idea that if you just explain things the right way, it’ll make him stop.
I’m here to let you know that not only is it not your job to give him feedback on his performance, you don’t have the ability to change what he does. Only he does. His superiors may have the power to enforce consequences if he doesn’t change but even they can’t control him. You are completely right for wanting out of there and rejecting his nonsense email. But on your way out, I’d do some self reflection on whether the tendency to take responsibility for your manager/supervisor is a norm this particular workplace enforced on you or if it’s a longer standing habit you’ve applied in other parts of life, that may follow you to future work places.
